       <tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;"> 
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox&nbsp;references</a></td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr style="background:#f8eaba;"> 
        <td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;"></td> 
        <td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;"></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p><b>Ammonia</b> or <b>azane</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compound</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">formula</a> NH<sub>3</sub>. It is a colourless <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with a characteristic pungent smell. Ammonia contributes significantly to the <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutritional</a> needs of terrestrial organisms by serving as a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>. Ammonia, either directly or indirectly, is also a building block for the synthesis of many <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" title="Pharmaceuticals" class="mw-redirect">pharmaceuticals</a> and is used in many commercial cleaning products. Although common in nature and in wide use, ammonia is both <a href="/wiki/Caustic_(substance)" title="Caustic (substance)" class="mw-redirect">caustic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hazard" title="Hazard">hazardous</a> in its concentrated form.</p> 
     <p>The global industrial production of ammonia in 2014 was 176,000,000 tonnes (173,000,000 long tons; 194,000,000 short tons),<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-10">[10]</a></sup> a 16% increase over the 2006 global industrial production of 152,000,000 tonnes (150,000,000 long tons; 168,000,000 short tons).<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016-ds140_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-ds140-11">[11]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>NH<sub>3</sub> boils at ‚àí33.34&nbsp;¬∞C (‚àí28.012&nbsp;¬∞F) at a pressure of one <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atmosphere</a>, so the liquid must be stored under pressure or at low temperature. Household ammonia or <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydroxide" title="Ammonium hydroxide">ammonium hydroxide</a> is a solution of NH<sub>3</sub> in water. The concentration of such solutions is measured in units of the <a href="/wiki/Baum%C3%A9_scale" title="Baum√© scale">Baum√© scale</a> (<a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>), with 26 degrees baum√© (about 30% (by weight) ammonia at 15.5&nbsp;¬∞C or 59.9&nbsp;¬∞F) being the typical high-concentration commercial product.<sup id="cite_ref-LaRoche_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaRoche-12">[12]</a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Natural occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Ammonia is found in trace quantities in the nature, being produced from the nitrogenous animal and vegetable matter. Ammonia and ammonium salts are also found in small quantities in rainwater, whereas <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sal_ammoniac" title="Sal ammoniac">sal ammoniac</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfate" title="Ammonium sulfate">ammonium sulfate</a> are found in volcanic districts; crystals of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_bicarbonate" title="Ammonium bicarbonate">ammonium bicarbonate</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> secrete ammonia to neutralize excess acid.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Ammonium salts are found distributed through fertile soil and in seawater.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia is also found throughout the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>. Substances containing ammonia, or those that are similar to it, are called <i>ammoniacal</i>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Ammonia is a colourless <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with a characteristic pungent smell. It is <a href="/wiki/Lighter_than_air" title="Lighter than air">lighter than air</a>, its density being 0.589 times that of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere" class="mw-redirect">air</a>. It is easily liquefied due to the strong <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonding</a> between molecules; the <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> boils at ‚àí33.3&nbsp;¬∞C (‚àí27.94&nbsp;¬∞F), and freezes at ‚àí77.7&nbsp;¬∞C (‚àí107.86&nbsp;¬∞F) to white crystals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-14">[14]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ammonia may be conveniently deodorized by reacting it with either sodium bicarbonate or acetic acid. Both of these reactions form an odourless ammonium salt.</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dt>
       Solid
      </dt> 
      <dd>
       The crystal symmetry is cubic, 
       <a href="/wiki/Pearson_symbol" title="Pearson symbol">Pearson symbol</a> cP16, 
       <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> P2
       <sub>1</sub>3 No.198, lattice constant 0.5125&nbsp;
       <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>.
       <sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <dl> 
      <dt>
       Liquid
      </dt> 
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">Liquid</a> ammonia possesses strong 
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionising</a> powers reflecting its high 
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant" class="mw-redirect">Œµ</a> of 22. Liquid ammonia has a very high 
       <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_vaporization" title="Enthalpy of vaporization">standard enthalpy change of vaporization</a> (23.35&nbsp;
       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">kJ</a>/mol, 
       <i>cf.</i> 
       <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a> 40.65&nbsp;kJ/mol, methane 8.19&nbsp;kJ/mol, 
       <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a> 14.6&nbsp;kJ/mol) and can therefore be used in laboratories in uninsulated vessels without additional refrigeration. See 
       <a href="#Liquid_ammonia_as_a_solvent">liquid ammonia as a solvent</a>.
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <dl> 
      <dt>
       Solvent properties
      </dt> 
      <dd>
       Ammonia is 
       <a href="/wiki/Miscible" title="Miscible" class="mw-redirect">miscible</a> with water. In an aqueous solution, it can be expelled by boiling. The 
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">aqueous</a> solution of ammonia is 
       <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a>. The maximum concentration of ammonia in water (a 
       <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(chemistry)" title="Saturation (chemistry)">saturated</a> solution) has a 
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of 0.880 g/cm
       <sup>3</sup> and is often known as '.880 ammonia'. Ammonia does not burn readily or sustain 
       <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, except under narrow fuel-to-air mixtures of 15‚Äì25% air.
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <dl> 
      <dt>
       Combustion
      </dt> 
      <dd>
       When mixed with 
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, it burns with a pale yellowish-green flame. At high temperature and in the presence of a suitable catalyst, ammonia is decomposed into its constituent elements. Ignition occurs when 
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> is passed into ammonia, forming nitrogen and 
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a>; if chlorine is present in excess, then the highly explosive 
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trichloride" title="Nitrogen trichloride">nitrogen trichloride</a> (NCl
       <sub>3</sub>) is also formed.
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The ammonia molecule has a <a href="/wiki/Trigonal_pyramid_(chemistry)" title="Trigonal pyramid (chemistry)" class="mw-redirect">trigonal pyramidal</a> shape as predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Valence_shell_electron_pair_repulsion_theory" title="Valence shell electron pair repulsion theory" class="mw-redirect">valence shell electron pair repulsion theory</a> (VSEPR theory) with an experimentally determined bond angle of 106.7¬∞.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_94th_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_94th-16">[16]</a></sup> The central nitrogen atom has five outer electrons with an additional electron from each hydrogen atom. This gives a total of eight electrons, or four electron pairs that are arranged tetrahedrally. Three of these electron pairs are used as bond pairs, which leaves one lone pair of electrons. The lone pair of electrons repel more strongly than bond pairs, therefore the bond angle is not 109.5¬∞, as expected for a regular tetrahedral arrangement, but 106.7¬∞.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_94th_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_94th-16">[16]</a></sup> The nitrogen atom in the molecule has a <a href="/wiki/Lone_pair" title="Lone pair">lone electron pair</a>, which makes ammonia a <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a>, a proton acceptor. This shape gives the molecule a <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a> moment and makes it <a href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule" class="mw-redirect">polar</a>. The molecule's polarity and, especially, its ability to form <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a>, makes ammonia highly miscible with <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. Ammonia is moderately basic, a 1.0 M aqueous solution has a pH of 11.6 and if a strong acid is added to such a solution until the solution is neutral (pH = 7), 99.4% of the ammonia molecules are <a href="/wiki/Protonation" title="Protonation">protonated</a>. Temperature and <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> also affect the proportion of NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>. The latter has the shape of a regular <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedron</a> and is <a href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic" class="mw-redirect">isoelectronic</a> with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.</p> 
     <p>The ammonia molecule readily undergoes <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_inversion" title="Nitrogen inversion">nitrogen inversion</a> at room temperature; a useful analogy is an <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrella</a> turning itself inside out in a strong wind. The energy barrier to this inversion is 24.7 kJ/mol, and the <a href="/wiki/Resonance_frequency" title="Resonance frequency" class="mw-redirect">resonance frequency</a> is 23.79 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">GHz</a>, corresponding to <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> radiation of a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of 1.260&nbsp;cm. The absorption at this frequency was the first <a href="/wiki/Microwave_spectroscopy" title="Microwave spectroscopy" class="mw-redirect">microwave spectrum</a> to be observed.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleeton_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleeton-17">[17]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphotericity">Amphotericity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Amphotericity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>One of the most characteristic properties of ammonia is its <a href="/wiki/Basicity" title="Basicity" class="mw-redirect">basicity</a>. Ammonia is considered to be a weak base. It combines with <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>; thus with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> it forms <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a> (sal ammoniac); with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a>, etc. Perfectly dry ammonia will not combine with perfectly dry <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a>; moisture is necessary to bring about the reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> As a demonstration experiment, opened bottles of concentrated ammonia and hydrochloric acid produce clouds of ammonium chloride, which seem to appear "out of nothing" as the salt forms where the two diffusing clouds of molecules meet, somewhere between the two bottles.</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       NH
       <sub>3</sub> + HCl ‚Üí NH
       <sub>4</sub>Cl
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The salts produced by the action of ammonia on acids are known as the <a href="/wiki/Category:Ammonium_compounds" title="Category:Ammonium compounds">ammonium salts</a> and all contain the <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium ion</a> (NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>).</p> 
     <p>Although ammonia is well known as a weak base, it can also act as an extremely weak acid. It is a protic substance and is capable of formation of <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amides</a> (which contain the NH<sub>2</sub><sup>‚àí</sup> ion). For example, <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> dissolves in liquid ammonia to give a solution of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_amide" title="Lithium amide">lithium amide</a>:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       2Li + 2NH
       <sub>3</sub> ‚Üí 2LiNH
       <sub>2</sub> + H
       <sub>2</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-dissociation">Self-dissociation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Self-dissociation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Like water, ammonia undergoes <a href="/wiki/Molecular_autoionisation" title="Molecular autoionisation" class="mw-redirect">molecular autoionisation</a> to form its acid and base conjugates:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       2 
       <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br> 3</span></span> (aq) 
       <img alt="is in equilibrium with" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Equilibrium.svg/15px-Equilibrium.svg.png" width="15" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Equilibrium.svg/23px-Equilibrium.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Equilibrium.svg/30px-Equilibrium.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="139" data-file-height="119"> 
       <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">+<br> 4</span></span> (aq) + 
       <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">‚àí<br> 2</span></span> (aq)
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>At standard pressure and temperature, K=[<span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">+<br> 4</span></span>][<span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">‚àí<br> 2</span></span>] = 10<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">‚àí30<br></span></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Combustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The combustion of ammonia to nitrogen and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic" class="mw-redirect">exothermic</a>:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       4 NH
       <sub>3</sub> + 3 O
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí 2 N
       <sub>2</sub> + 6 H
       <sub>2</sub>O (
       <i>g</i>) (
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_of_reaction" title="Standard enthalpy of reaction">Œî<i>H</i>¬∞<sub>r</sub></a> = ‚àí1267.20 kJ/mol)
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_change_of_combustion" title="Standard enthalpy change of combustion" class="mw-redirect">standard enthalpy change of combustion</a>, Œî<i>H</i>¬∞<sub>c</sub>, expressed per <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> of ammonia and with condensation of the water formed, is ‚àí382.81&nbsp;kJ/mol. Dinitrogen is the thermodynamic product of combustion: all <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a> are unstable with respect to N<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub>, which is the principle behind the <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">catalytic converter</a>. Nitrogen oxides can be formed as kinetic products in the presence of appropriate catalysts, a reaction of great industrial importance in the production of <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       4 NH
       <sub>3</sub> + 5 O
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí 4 NO + 6 H
       <sub>2</sub>O
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>A subsequent reaction leads to NO<sub>2</sub></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       2 NO + O
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí 2 NO
       <sub>2</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The combustion of ammonia in air is very difficult in the absence of a catalyst (such as <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> gauze or warm <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_oxide" title="Chromium(III) oxide">chromium(III) oxide</a>), because the temperature of the flame is usually lower than the ignition temperature of the ammonia‚Äìair mixture. The flammable range of ammonia in air is 16‚Äì25%.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_other_compounds">Formation of other compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Formation of other compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>, ammonia can act as a <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_substitution" title="Nucleophilic substitution">substitution</a> reactions. <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">Amines</a> can be formed by the reaction of ammonia with <a href="/wiki/Alkyl_halide" title="Alkyl halide" class="mw-redirect">alkyl halides</a>, although the resulting -NH<sub>2</sub> group is also nucleophilic and secondary and tertiary amines are often formed as byproducts. An excess of ammonia helps minimise multiple substitution, and neutralises the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halide</a> formed. <a href="/wiki/Methylamine" title="Methylamine">Methylamine</a> is prepared commercially by the reaction of ammonia with <a href="/wiki/Chloromethane" title="Chloromethane">chloromethane</a>, and the reaction of ammonia with 2-bromopropanoic acid has been used to prepare <a href="/wiki/Racemic" title="Racemic" class="mw-redirect">racemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Alanine" title="Alanine">alanine</a> in 70% yield. <a href="/wiki/Ethanolamine" title="Ethanolamine">Ethanolamine</a> is prepared by a ring-opening reaction with <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>: the reaction is sometimes allowed to go further to produce <a href="/wiki/Diethanolamine" title="Diethanolamine">diethanolamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triethanolamine" title="Triethanolamine">triethanolamine</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">Amides</a> can be prepared by the reaction of ammonia with <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a> derivatives. <a href="/wiki/Acyl_chloride" title="Acyl chloride">Acyl chlorides</a> are the most reactive, but the ammonia must be present in at least a twofold excess to neutralise the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> formed. <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">Esters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anhydride" title="Anhydride" class="mw-redirect">anhydrides</a> also react with ammonia to form amides. Ammonium salts of carboxylic acids can be <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydrated</a> to amides so long as there are no thermally sensitive groups present: temperatures of 150‚Äì200&nbsp;¬∞C are required.</p> 
     <p>The hydrogen in ammonia is capable of replacement by <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, thus <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> burns in the gas with the formation of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_nitride" title="Magnesium nitride">magnesium nitride</a> Mg<sub>3</sub>N<sub>2</sub>, and when the gas is passed over heated <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, sodamide, NaNH<sub>2</sub>, and potassamide, KNH<sub>2</sub>, are formed. Where necessary in <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature" title="IUPAC nomenclature" class="mw-redirect">substitutive nomenclature</a>, <a href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC" class="mw-redirect">IUPAC</a> recommendations prefer the name "azane" to ammonia: hence <a href="/wiki/Chloramine" title="Chloramine">chloramine</a> would be named "chloroazane" in substitutive nomenclature, not "chloroammonia".</p> 
     <p>Pentavalent ammonia is known as Œª<sup>5</sup>-amine, or more commonly, ammonium hydride. This crystalline solid is only stable under high pressure, and decomposes back into trivalent ammonia and hydrogen gas at normal conditions. This substance was once investigated as a possible solid rocket fuel in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ammonia_as_a_ligand">Ammonia as a ligand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ammonia as a ligand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Metal_ammine_complex" title="Metal ammine complex">Metal ammine complex</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/220px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/330px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/440px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="1100"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick model</a> of the tetraamminediaquacopper(II) cation, [Cu(NH
        <sub>3</sub>)
        <sub>4</sub>(H
        <sub>2</sub>O)
        <sub>2</sub>]
        <sup>2+</sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia can act as a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)" class="mw-redirect">complexes</a>. It is a pure œÉ-donor, in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Spectrochemical_series" title="Spectrochemical series">spectrochemical series</a>, and shows intermediate <a href="/wiki/HSAB_concept" title="HSAB concept" class="mw-redirect">hard-soft</a> behaviour. For historical reasons, ammonia is named <b>ammine</b> in the nomenclature of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_compound" title="Coordination compound" class="mw-redirect">coordination compounds</a>. Some notable ammine complexes include tetraamminediaquacopper(II) ([Cu(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>), a dark blue complex formed by adding ammonia to a solution of copper(II) salts. Tetraamminediaquacopper(II) hydroxide is known as <a href="/wiki/Schweizer%27s_reagent" title="Schweizer's reagent">Schweizer's reagent</a>, and has the remarkable ability to dissolve <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>. Diamminesilver(I) ([Ag(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>) is the active species in <a href="/wiki/Tollens%27_reagent" title="Tollens' reagent">Tollens' reagent</a>. Formation of this complex can also help to distinguish between precipitates of the different silver halides: <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a> (AgCl) is soluble in dilute (2M) ammonia solution, <a href="/wiki/Silver_bromide" title="Silver bromide">silver bromide</a> (AgBr) is only soluble in concentrated ammonia solution, whereas <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a> (AgI) is insoluble in aqueous ammonia.</p> 
     <p>Ammine complexes of <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>(III) were known in the late 19th century, and formed the basis of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Werner" title="Alfred Werner">Alfred Werner</a>'s revolutionary theory on the structure of coordination compounds. Werner noted only two isomers (<i>fac</i>- and <i>mer</i>-) of the complex [CrCl<sub>3</sub>(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>] could be formed, and concluded the ligands must be arranged around the metal ion at the <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vertex" class="extiw" title="wikt:vertex">vertices</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedron</a>. This proposal has since been confirmed by <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>.</p> 
     <p>An ammine ligand bound to a metal ion is markedly more acidic than a free ammonia molecule, although deprotonation in aqueous solution is still rare. One example is the <a href="/wiki/Mercury(I)_chloride" title="Mercury(I) chloride">Calomel reaction</a>, where the resulting amidomercury(II) compound is highly insoluble.</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       Hg
       <sub>2</sub>Cl
       <sub>2</sub> + 2 NH
       <sub>3</sub> ‚Üí Hg + HgCl(NH
       <sub>2</sub>) + NH
       <sub>4</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> + Cl
       <sup>‚àí</sup>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_and_determination">Detection and determination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Detection and determination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p><i>This section is about detection in the laboratory. For detection in astronomy, see chapter <a href="#In_astronomy">In astronomy</a>.</i></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ammonia_in_solution">Ammonia in solution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ammonia in solution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Ammonia and ammonium salts can be readily detected, in very minute traces, by the addition of <a href="/wiki/Nessler%27s_solution" title="Nessler's solution" class="mw-redirect">Nessler's solution</a>, which gives a distinct yellow colouration in the presence of the least trace of ammonia or ammonium salts. The amount of ammonia in ammonium salts can be estimated quantitatively by distillation of the salts with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, the ammonia evolved being absorbed in a known volume of standard <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> and the excess of acid then determined <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_analysis" title="Volumetric analysis" class="mw-redirect">volumetrically</a>; or the ammonia may be absorbed in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and the ammonium chloride so formed precipitated as <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hexachloroplatinate" title="Ammonium hexachloroplatinate">ammonium hexachloroplatinate</a>, (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>PtCl<sub>6</sub>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaseous_ammonia">Gaseous ammonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gaseous ammonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Sulfur_sticks" title="Sulfur sticks">Sulfur sticks</a> are burnt to detect small leaks in industrial ammonia refrigeration systems. Larger quantities can be detected by warming the salts with a caustic alkali or with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a>, when the characteristic smell of ammonia will be at once apparent. Ammonia is an irritant and irritation increases with concentration; the Permissible Exposure Limit is 25&nbsp;ppm, and lethal above 500&nbsp;ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Higher concentrations are hardly detected by conventional detectors, the type of detector is chosen according to the sensitivity required (e.g. semiconductor, catalytic, electrochemical). Holographic sensors have been proposed for detecting concentrations up to 12.5% in volume.<sup id="cite_ref-HurtadoLowe2014_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HurtadoLowe2014-22">[22]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ammoniacal_nitrogen_.28NH3-N.29">Ammoniacal nitrogen (NH<sub>3</sub>-N)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ammoniacal nitrogen (NH3-N)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Ammoniacal_nitrogen" title="Ammoniacal nitrogen">Ammoniacal nitrogen</a> (NH<sub>3</sub>-N) is a measure commonly used for testing the quantity of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> ions, derived naturally from ammonia, and returned to ammonia via organic processes, in water or waste liquids. It is a measure used mainly for quantifying values in waste treatment and water purification systems, as well as a measure of the health of natural and man made water reserves. It is measured in units of mg/L (<a href="/wiki/Milligram" title="Milligram" class="mw-redirect">milligram</a> per <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a>).</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg/170px-Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg/255px-Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg/340px-Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1999"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Ammoniak_Reaktor_BASF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> This high-pressure reactor was built in 1921 by 
        <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Ludwigshafen" title="Ludwigshafen">Ludwigshafen</a> and was re-erected on the premises of the 
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Karlsruhe" title="University of Karlsruhe" class="mw-redirect">University of Karlsruhe</a> in Germany.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Romans gave the name <i>sal ammoniacus</i> (salt of Amun) to the ammonium chloride deposits that they collected near the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ·ºåŒºŒºœâŒΩ <i>Ammon</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">ancient Libya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Salts of ammonia have been known from very early times; thus the term <i>Hammoniacus sal</i> appears in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> although it is not known whether the term is identical with the more modern sal ammoniac (ammonium chloride).<sup id="cite_ref-Mineral_Data_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mineral_Data-25">[25]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the form of sal ammoniac <i>(ŸÜÿ¥ÿßÿØÿ±, nushadir)</i> ammonia was important to the <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">Muslim alchemists</a> as early as the 8th century, first mentioned by the Persian chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C4%81bir_ibn_Hayy%C4%81n" title="JƒÅbir ibn HayyƒÅn" class="mw-redirect">JƒÅbir ibn HayyƒÅn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Haq1995_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haq1995-26">[26]</a></sup> and to the European <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a> since the 13th century, being mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-14">[14]</a></sup> It was also used by <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> in the form of fermented <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> to alter the colour of vegetable dyes. In the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Basilius_Valentinus" title="Basilius Valentinus" class="mw-redirect">Basilius Valentinus</a> showed that ammonia could be obtained by the action of alkalis on sal ammoniac. At a later period, when sal ammoniac was obtained by distilling the hooves and horns of oxen and neutralizing the resulting carbonate with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, the name "spirit of hartshorn" was applied to ammonia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Gaseous ammonia was first isolated by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> in 1774 and was termed by him "alkaline air".<sup id="cite_ref-Alchemy_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alchemy-28">[28]</a></sup> Eleven years later in 1785, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Louis_Berthollet" title="Claude Louis Berthollet">Claude Louis Berthollet</a> ascertained its composition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-14">[14]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Haber%E2%80%93Bosch_process" title="Haber‚ÄìBosch process" class="mw-redirect">Haber‚ÄìBosch process</a> to produce ammonia from the nitrogen in the air was developed by <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Haber" title="Fritz Haber">Fritz Haber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bosch" title="Carl Bosch">Carl Bosch</a> in 1909 and patented in 1910. It was first used on an industrial scale in Germany during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-29">[29]</a></sup> following the allied blockade that cut off the supply of nitrates from <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>. The ammonia was used to produce explosives to sustain war efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-Conquering_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conquering-30">[30]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Prior to the availability of natural gas, hydrogen as a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia production</a> was produced via the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of water or using the <a href="/wiki/Chloralkali_process" title="Chloralkali process">chloralkali process</a>.</p> 
     <p>With the advent of the steel industry in the 20th century, ammonia became a byproduct of the production of coking coal.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizer">Fertilizer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fertilizer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Globally, approximately 88% (as of 2014) of ammonia is used as fertilizers either as its salts, solutions or anhydrously.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-10">[10]</a></sup> When applied to soil, it helps provide increased yields of crops such as maize and wheat.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 30% of agricultural nitrogen applied in the USA is in the form of anhydrous ammonia and worldwide 110 million tonnes are applied each year.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_nitrogenous_compounds">Precursor to nitrogenous compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Precursor to nitrogenous compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Ammonia is directly or indirectly the precursor to most nitrogen-containing compounds. Virtually all synthetic nitrogen compounds are derived from ammonia. An important derivative is <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>. This key material is generated via the <a href="/wiki/Ostwald_process" title="Ostwald process">Ostwald process</a> by <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation" class="mw-redirect">oxidation</a> of ammonia with air over a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> catalyst at 700‚Äì850&nbsp;¬∞C (1,292‚Äì1,562&nbsp;¬∞F), ~9 atm. <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">Nitric oxide</a> is an intermediate in this conversion:<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       NH
       <sub>3</sub> + 2 O
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí HNO
       <sub>3</sub> + H
       <sub>2</sub>O
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Nitric acid is used for the production of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive" class="mw-redirect">explosives</a>, and many organonitrogen compounds.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia is also used to make the following compounds:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">Hydrazine</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Olin_Raschig_process" title="Olin Raschig process">Olin Raschig process</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peroxide_process" title="Peroxide process">peroxide process</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">Hydrogen cyanide</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/BMA_process" title="BMA process">BMA process</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Andrussow_process" title="Andrussow process">Andrussow process</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Hydroxylamine" title="Hydroxylamine">Hydroxylamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_carbonate" title="Ammonium carbonate">ammonium carbonate</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Raschig_process" title="Raschig process">Raschig process</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">Phenol</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Raschig%E2%80%93Hooker_process" title="Raschig‚ÄìHooker process">Raschig‚ÄìHooker process</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">Urea</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Bosch%E2%80%93Meiser_urea_process" title="Bosch‚ÄìMeiser urea process" class="mw-redirect">Bosch‚ÄìMeiser urea process</a> and in <a href="/wiki/W%C3%B6hler_synthesis" title="W√∂hler synthesis">W√∂hler synthesis</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acids</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Strecker_amino-acid_synthesis" title="Strecker amino-acid synthesis" class="mw-redirect">Strecker amino-acid synthesis</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Acrylonitrile" title="Acrylonitrile">Acrylonitrile</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Sohio_process" title="Sohio process" class="mw-redirect">Sohio process</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Ammonia can also be used to make compounds in reactions which are not specifically named. Examples of such compounds include: <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate" title="Ammonium perchlorate">ammonium perchlorate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formamide" title="Formamide">formamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinitrogen_tetroxide" title="Dinitrogen tetroxide">dinitrogen tetroxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alprazolam" title="Alprazolam">alprazolam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanolamine" title="Ethanolamine">ethanolamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_carbamate" title="Ethyl carbamate">ethyl carbamate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexamethylenetetramine" title="Hexamethylenetetramine">hexamethylenetetramine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_bicarbonate" title="Ammonium bicarbonate">ammonium bicarbonate</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleaner">Cleaner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cleaner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Household ammonia is a solution of NH<sub>3</sub> in water (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydroxide" title="Ammonium hydroxide">ammonium hydroxide</a>) used as a general purpose cleaner for many surfaces. Because ammonia results in a relatively streak-free shine, one of its most common uses is to clean glass, porcelain and stainless steel. It is also frequently used for cleaning ovens and soaking items to loosen baked-on grime. Household ammonia ranges in concentration by weight from 5 to 10% ammonia.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation">Fermentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fermentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Solutions of ammonia ranging from 16% to 25% are used in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_fermentation" title="Industrial fermentation">fermentation</a> industry as a source of nitrogen for microorganisms and to adjust pH during fermentation.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimicrobial_agent_for_food_products">Antimicrobial agent for food products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Antimicrobial agent for food products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>As early as in 1895, it was known that ammonia was "strongly <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> ... it requires 1.4 grams per litre to preserve <a href="/wiki/Beef_tea" title="Beef tea" class="mw-redirect">beef tea</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In one study, anhydrous ammonia destroyed 99.999% of <a href="/wiki/Zoonotic_bacteria" title="Zoonotic bacteria" class="mw-redirect">zoonotic bacteria</a> in 3 types of <a href="/wiki/Compound_feed" title="Compound feed" class="mw-redirect">animal feed</a>, but not <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary.2C_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (July 2013)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Anhydrous ammonia is currently used commercially to reduce or eliminate <a href="/wiki/Microbial" title="Microbial" class="mw-redirect">microbial</a> contamination of <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Lean finely textured beef in the beef industry is made from fatty <a href="/wiki/Beef_trimmings" title="Beef trimmings" class="mw-redirect">beef trimmings</a> (about 50‚Äì70% fat) by removing the fat using heat and <a href="/wiki/Centrifugation" title="Centrifugation">centrifugation</a>, then treating it with ammonia to kill <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i>. The process was deemed effective and safe by the <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Agriculture" title="US Department of Agriculture" class="mw-redirect">US Department of Agriculture</a> based on a study that found that the treatment reduces <i>E. coli</i> to undetectable levels.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> There have been safety concerns about the process as well as consumer complaints about the taste and smell of beef treated at optimal levels of ammonia.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The level of ammonia in any final product has not come close to toxic levels to humans.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_and_emerging_uses">Minor and emerging uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Minor and emerging uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigeration_.E2.80.93_R717">Refrigeration ‚Äì R717</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Refrigeration ‚Äì R717">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Because of ammonia's vaporization properties, it is a useful <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-29">[29]</a></sup> It was commonly used prior to the popularisation of <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbons</a> (Freons). Anhydrous ammonia is widely used in industrial refrigeration applications and hockey rinks because of its high <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">energy efficiency</a> and low cost. It suffers from the disadvantage of toxicity, which restricts its domestic and small-scale use. Along with its use in modern <a href="/wiki/Vapor-compression_refrigeration" title="Vapor-compression refrigeration">vapor-compression refrigeration</a> it is used in a mixture along with hydrogen and water in <a href="/wiki/Absorption_refrigerator" title="Absorption refrigerator">absorption refrigerators</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kalina_cycle" title="Kalina cycle">Kalina cycle</a>, which is of growing importance to geothermal power plants, depends on the wide boiling range of the ammonia‚Äìwater mixture.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="For_remediation_of_gaseous_emissions">For remediation of gaseous emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: For remediation of gaseous emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Ammonia is used to scrub SO<sub>2</sub> from the burning of fossil fuels, and the resulting product is converted to ammonium sulfate for use as fertilizer. Ammonia neutralizes the nitrogen oxides (NO<sub>x</sub>) pollutants emitted by diesel engines. This technology, called SCR (<a href="/wiki/Selective_catalytic_reduction" title="Selective catalytic reduction">selective catalytic reduction</a>), relies on a <a href="/wiki/Vanadia" title="Vanadia" class="mw-redirect">vanadia</a>-based catalyst.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><br> Ammonia may be used to mitigate gaseous spills of <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_fuel">As a fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: As a fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content ambox-disputed" role="presentation">

     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg/220px-AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg/330px-AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg/440px-AmmoniacalGasEngineStreetcarARWaud.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="570"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">

        </div> Ammoniacal Gas Engine 
        <a href="/wiki/Streetcars_in_New_Orleans" title="Streetcars in New Orleans">Streetcar in New Orleans</a> drawn by 
        <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Waud" title="Alfred Waud">Alfred Waud</a> in 1871.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:X-15.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/X-15.jpg/220px-X-15.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/X-15.jpg/330px-X-15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/X-15.jpg/440px-X-15.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1934"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:X-15.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15" class="mw-redirect">X-15</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> used ammonia as one component 
        <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> of its 
        <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Engine" title="Engine">engine</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia was used during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> to power buses in Belgium, and in engine and solar energy applications prior to 1900. Liquid ammonia also fuelled the <a href="/wiki/Reaction_Motors_XLR99" title="Reaction Motors XLR99">Reaction Motors XLR99</a> rocket engine that powered the <a href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15" class="mw-redirect">X-15</a> hypersonic research aircraft. Although not as powerful as other fuels, it left no soot in the reusable rocket engine and its density approximately matches the density of the oxidizer, liquid oxygen, which simplified the aircraft's design.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia has been proposed as a practical alternative to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> for <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engines" title="Internal combustion engines" class="mw-redirect">internal combustion engines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-c1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c1-41">[41]</a></sup> The calorific value of ammonia is 22.5 MJ/kg (9690 <a href="/wiki/BTU" title="BTU" class="mw-redirect">BTU</a>/lb), which is about half that of diesel. In a normal engine, in which the water vapour is not condensed, the calorific value of ammonia will be about 21% less than this figure.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia cannot be easily used in existing <a href="/wiki/Otto_cycle" title="Otto cycle">Otto cycle</a> engines because of its very narrow <a href="/wiki/Ammonia#Combustion" title="Ammonia">flammability range</a>, although its stellar octane rating of 120<sup id="cite_ref-Chemistry_department.2C_University_of_Bristol_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chemistry_department.2C_University_of_Bristol-42">[42]</a></sup> and low flame temperature allows the use of high compression ratios without a penalty of high NOx production.&nbsp; Since ammonia contains no carbon, its combustion cannot produce carbon monoxide, hydrocarbons or soot.&nbsp; The conversion of ammonia to hydrogen via the sodium-amide process,<sup id="cite_ref-American_Chemical_Society_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Chemical_Society-43">[43]</a></sup> either as a catalyst for combustion or as fuel for a <a href="/wiki/Proton_exchange_membrane_fuel_cell" title="Proton exchange membrane fuel cell">proton exchange membrane fuel cell</a>, is another possibility.&nbsp; Conversion to hydrogen would allow the storage of hydrogen at nearly 18 wt% compared to ~5% for gaseous hydrogen under pressure.</p> 
     <p>An automobile fuel tank could store ammonia as a liquid as long as the tank was pressurized appropriately, depending on the temperature. Ammonia's <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_(data_page)#Thermodynamic_properties" title="Ammonia (data page)">thermodynamic properties</a> are such that at -30&nbsp;¬∞C, the tank pressure would only have to be 27.5 psi, about the same as a car tire. At 30&nbsp;¬∞C (86&nbsp;¬∞F) the pressure in the tank would need to be 170 psi to keep the ammonia liquid. If tank pressure was released, the liquid ammonia would turn gaseous and raise the pressure again to that level. Common pneumatic tool air compressors operate at this pressure, so fuel tank pressure is not a barrier to adoption of automobile fuel usage.</p> 
     <p>There are other barriers to widespread automobile usage. In terms of raw ammonia supplies, plants would have to be built to increase production levels, requiring significant capital and energy sources. Although it is the second most produced chemical, the scale of ammonia production is a small fraction of world petroleum usage. It could be manufactured from renewable energy sources, as well as coal or nuclear power. The 60 MW Rjukan dam in <a href="/wiki/Telemark" title="Telemark">Telemark</a>, Norway produced ammonia via electrolysis of water for many years from 1913 producing fertilizer for much of Europe. If produced from coal, the CO<sub>2</sub> could be sequestered, but <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> from coal power plants is not yet beyond prototype stages.</p> 
     <p>In 1981, a Canadian company converted a 1981 Chevrolet Impala to operate using ammonia as fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2007, a University of Michigan pickup powered by ammonia drove from Detroit to San Francisco as part of a demonstration, requiring only one fill-up in Wyoming.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ammonia engines or ammonia motors, using ammonia as a <a href="/wiki/Working_fluid" title="Working fluid">working fluid</a>, have been proposed and occasionally used.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The principle is similar to that used in a <a href="/wiki/Fireless_locomotive" title="Fireless locomotive">fireless locomotive</a>, but with ammonia as the working fluid, instead of steam or compressed air. Ammonia engines were used experimentally in the 19th century by <a href="/wiki/Goldsworthy_Gurney" title="Goldsworthy Gurney">Goldsworthy Gurney</a> in the UK and in <a href="/wiki/Streetcars_in_New_Orleans" title="Streetcars in New Orleans">streetcars in New Orleans</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_stimulant">As a stimulant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: As a stimulant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG/220px-Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG/330px-Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG/440px-Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Meth_ammonia_tank_Otley_iowa.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Anti-
        <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">meth</a> sign on tank of anhydrous ammonia, 
        <a href="/wiki/Otley,_Iowa" title="Otley, Iowa">Otley, Iowa</a>. Anhydrous ammonia is a common farm fertilizer that is also a critical ingredient in making methamphetamine. In 2005, Iowa state used grant money to give out thousands of locks to prevent criminals from getting into the tanks.
        <sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia, as the vapor released by <a href="/wiki/Smelling_salts" title="Smelling salts">smelling salts</a>, has found significant use as a respiratory stimulant. Ammonia is commonly used in the illegal manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a> through a <a href="/wiki/Birch_reduction" title="Birch reduction">Birch reduction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Birch method of making methamphetamine is dangerous because the alkali metal and liquid ammonia are both extremely reactive, and the temperature of liquid ammonia makes it susceptible to explosive boiling when reactants are added.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile">Textile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Textile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Liquid ammonia is used for treatment of cotton materials, giving properties like <a href="/wiki/Mercerisation" title="Mercerisation" class="mw-redirect">mercerisation</a>, using alkalis. In particular, it is used for prewashing of wool.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifting_gas">Lifting gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Lifting gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>At standard temperature and pressure, ammonia is less dense than atmosphere, and has approximately 60% of the lifting power of hydrogen or <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Ammonia has sometimes been used to fill weather balloons as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a>. Because of its relatively high boiling point (compared to helium and hydrogen), ammonia could potentially be refrigerated and liquefied aboard an <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a> to reduce lift and add ballast (and returned to a gas to add lift and reduce ballast).</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Woodworking">Woodworking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Woodworking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_fuming" title="Ammonia fuming">Ammonia fuming</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia has been used to darken quartersawn white oak in Arts &amp; Crafts and Mission-style furniture. Ammonia fumes react with the natural tannins in the wood and cause it to change colours.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_precautions">Safety precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Safety precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg/220px-Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg" width="220" height="337" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg/330px-Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg/440px-Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="522" data-file-height="800"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Ammiakoprovod_NS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The world's longest ammonia 
        <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline</a>, running from the 
        <a href="/wiki/TogliattiAzot" title="TogliattiAzot">TogliattiAzot</a> plant in 
        <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to 
        <a href="/wiki/Odessa" title="Odessa">Odessa</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The U. S. <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration (OSHA)</a> has set a 15-minute exposure limit for gaseous ammonia of 35 ppm by volume in the environmental air and an 8-hour exposure limit of 25 ppm by volume.<sup id="cite_ref-ToxFaqSheet_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ToxFaqSheet-52">[52]</a></sup> NIOSH recently reduced the IDLH from 500 to 300 based on recent more conservative interpretations of original research in 1943. IDLH (Immediately Dangerous to Life and Health) is the level to which a healthy worker can be exposed for 30 minutes without suffering irreversible health effects. Other organizations have varying exposure levels. U.S. Navy Standards [U.S. Bureau of Ships 1962] maximum allowable concentrations (MACs):continuous exposure (60 days): 25 ppm / 1 hour: 400 ppm<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Ammonia vapour has a sharp, irritating, pungent odour that acts as a warning of potentially dangerous exposure. The average odour threshold is 5 ppm, well below any danger or damage. Exposure to very high concentrations of gaseous ammonia can result in lung damage and death.<sup id="cite_ref-ToxFaqSheet_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ToxFaqSheet-52">[52]</a></sup> Although ammonia is regulated in the United States as a non-flammable gas, it still meets the definition of a material that is toxic by inhalation and requires a hazardous safety permit when transported in quantities greater than 13,248 L (3,500 gallons).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The toxicity of ammonia solutions does not usually cause problems for humans and other mammals, as a specific mechanism exists to prevent its build-up in the bloodstream. Ammonia is converted to <a href="/wiki/Carbamoyl_phosphate" title="Carbamoyl phosphate">carbamoyl phosphate</a> by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbamoyl_phosphate_synthetase" title="Carbamoyl phosphate synthetase">carbamoyl phosphate synthetase</a>, and then enters the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a> to be either incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> or excreted in the urine<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">Fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> lack this mechanism, as they can usually eliminate ammonia from their bodies by direct excretion. Ammonia even at dilute concentrations is highly toxic to aquatic animals, and for this reason it is <a href="/wiki/Directive_67/548/EEC" title="Directive 67/548/EEC" class="mw-redirect">classified</a> as <i>dangerous for the environment</i>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coking_wastewater">Coking wastewater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Coking wastewater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Ammonia is present in coking wastewater streams, as a liquid by-product of the production of <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> from <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In some cases, the ammonia is discharged to the <a href="/wiki/Marine_environment" title="Marine environment" class="mw-redirect">marine environment</a> where it acts as a pollutant. The <a href="/wiki/Whyalla_steelworks" title="Whyalla steelworks" class="mw-redirect">Whyalla steelworks</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> is one example of a coke-producing facility which discharges ammonia into marine waters.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Aquaculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Ammonia toxicity is believed to be a cause of otherwise unexplained losses in fish hatcheries. Excess ammonia may accumulate and cause alteration of metabolism or increases in the body pH of the exposed organism. Tolerance varies among fish species.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-56">[56]</a></sup> At lower concentrations, around 0.05&nbsp;mg/L, un-ionised ammonia is harmful to fish species and can result in poor growth and feed conversion rates, reduced fecundity and fertility and increase stress and susceptibility to bacterial infections and diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Exposed to excess ammonia, fish may suffer loss of equilibrium, hyper-excitability, increased respiratory activity and oxygen uptake and increased heart rate.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-56">[56]</a></sup> At concentrations exceeding 2.0&nbsp;mg/L, ammonia causes gill and tissue damage, extreme lethargy, convulsions, coma, and death.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup> Experiments have shown that the lethal concentration for a variety of fish species ranges from 0.2 to 2.0&nbsp;mg/l.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>During winter, when reduced feeds are administered to aquaculture stock, ammonia levels can be higher. Lower ambient temperatures reduce the rate of algal photosynthesis so less ammonia is removed by any algae present. Within an aquaculture environment, especially at large scale, there is no fast-acting remedy to elevated ammonia levels. Prevention rather than correction is recommended to reduce harm to farmed fish<sup id="cite_ref-:1_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup> and in open water systems, the surrounding environment.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_information">Storage information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Storage information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Similar to <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> ammonia boils below room temperature when at atmospheric pressure. A storage vessel capable of 250&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a> (1.7&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>) is suitable to contain the liquid.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Ammonium compounds should never be allowed to come in contact with <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">bases</a> (unless in an intended and contained reaction), as dangerous quantities of ammonia gas could be released.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Household_use">Household use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Household use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Solutions of ammonia (5‚Äì10% by weight) are used as household cleaners, particularly for glass. These solutions are irritating to the eyes and <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a> (respiratory and digestive tracts), and to a lesser extent the skin. Caution should be used that the chemical is never mixed into any liquid containing bleach, as a poisonous gas may result. Mixing with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>-containing products or strong oxidants, such as household <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a>, can lead to hazardous compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Chloramine" title="Chloramine">chloramines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_use_of_ammonia_solutions">Laboratory use of ammonia solutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Laboratory use of ammonia solutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg/220px-Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="301" data-file-height="296"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hydrochloric_acid_ammonia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hydrochloric acid sample releasing HCl fumes, which are reacting with ammonia fumes to produce a white smoke of ammonium chloride.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The hazards of ammonia solutions depend on the concentration: "dilute" ammonia solutions are usually 5‚Äì10% by weight (&lt;5.62&nbsp;mol/L); "concentrated" solutions are usually prepared at &gt;25% by weight. A 25% (by weight) solution has a density of 0.907 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, and a solution that has a lower density will be more concentrated. The <a href="/wiki/Directive_67/548/EEC" title="Directive 67/548/EEC" class="mw-redirect">European Union classification</a> of ammonia solutions is given in the table.</p> 
     <table class="wikitable"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <th><a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">Concentration</a><br> by weight (w/w)</th>

        <th><a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">Concentration</a><br> mass/volume (w/v)</th> 
        <th>Classification</th>

       </tr>

       </tr> 
       <tr>
