       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_S-phrases" title="List of S-phrases">S-Phrases</a>: <a href="/wiki/(S1/2):_Keep_locked_up_and_out_of_the_reach_of_children" title="(S1/2): Keep locked up and out of the reach of children" class="mw-redirect"><abbr class="abbr" title="(S1/2): Keep locked up and out of the reach of children">(S1/2)</abbr></a>, <a href="/wiki/S16:_Keep_away_from_sources_of_ignition_-_No_smoking" title="S16: Keep away from sources of ignition - No smoking" class="mw-redirect"><abbr class="abbr" title="S16: Keep away from sources of ignition - No smoking">S16</abbr></a>, <a href="/wiki/S36/37/39:_Wear_suitable_protective_clothing,_gloves_and_eye/face_protection" title="S36/37/39: Wear suitable protective clothing, gloves and eye/face protection" class="mw-redirect"><abbr class="abbr" title="S36/37/39: Wear suitable protective clothing, gloves and eye/face protection">S36/37/39</abbr></a>, <a href="/wiki/S45:_In_case_of_accident_or_if_you_feel_unwell_seek_medical_advice_immediately_(show_the_label_where_possible)" title="S45: In case of accident or if you feel unwell seek medical advice immediately (show the label where possible)" class="mw-redirect"><abbr class="abbr" title="S45: In case of accident or if you feel unwell seek medical advice immediately (show the label where possible)">S45</abbr></a>, <a href="/wiki/S61:_Avoid_release_to_the_environment._Refer_to_special_instructions/safety_data_sheet" title="S61: Avoid release to the environment. Refer to special instructions/safety data sheet" class="mw-redirect"><abbr class="abbr" title="S61: Avoid release to the environment. Refer to special instructions/safety data sheet">S61</abbr></a>.</i>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The ammonia vapour from concentrated ammonia solutions is severely irritating to the eyes and the respiratory tract, and these solutions should only be handled in a fume hood. Saturated ("0.880") solutions can develop a significant pressure inside a closed bottle in warm weather, and the bottle should be opened with care; this is not usually a problem for 25% ("0.900") solutions.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia solutions should not be mixed with <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, as toxic and/or explosive products are formed. Prolonged contact of ammonia solutions with <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> or <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> salts can also lead to explosive products: such mixtures are often formed in <a href="/wiki/Qualitative_inorganic_analysis" title="Qualitative inorganic analysis">qualitative inorganic analysis</a>, and should be lightly acidified but not concentrated (&lt;6% w/v) before disposal once the test is completed.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_use_of_anhydrous_ammonia_.28gas_or_liquid.29">Laboratory use of anhydrous ammonia (gas or liquid)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Laboratory use of anhydrous ammonia (gas or liquid)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Anhydrous ammonia is classified as toxic (<b>T</b>) and dangerous for the environment (<b>N</b>). The gas is flammable (<a href="/wiki/Autoignition_temperature" title="Autoignition temperature">autoignition temperature</a>: 651&nbsp;¬∞C) and can form explosive mixtures with air (16‚Äì25%). The <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) in the United States is 50&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million" class="mw-redirect">ppm</a> (35&nbsp;mg/m<sup>3</sup>), while the <a href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH" class="mw-redirect">IDLH</a> concentration is estimated at 300&nbsp;ppm. Repeated exposure to ammonia lowers the sensitivity to the smell of the gas: normally the odour is detectable at concentrations of less than 50&nbsp;ppm, but desensitised individuals may not detect it even at concentrations of 100&nbsp;ppm. Anhydrous ammonia corrodes <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>, and so <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> fittings should not be used for handling the gas. Liquid ammonia can also attack <a href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber" class="mw-redirect">rubber</a> and certain plastics.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia reacts violently with the halogens. <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_triiodide" title="Nitrogen triiodide">Nitrogen triiodide</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Primary_explosive" title="Primary explosive" class="mw-redirect">primary</a> <a href="/wiki/High_explosive" title="High explosive" class="mw-redirect">high explosive</a>, is formed when ammonia comes in contact with <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>. Ammonia causes the explosive <a href="/wiki/Polymerisation" title="Polymerisation" class="mw-redirect">polymerisation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>. It also forms explosive <a href="/wiki/Fulminating" title="Fulminating" class="mw-redirect">fulminating</a> compounds with compounds of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a>, and with <a href="/wiki/Stibine" title="Stibine">stibine</a>. Violent reactions have also been reported with <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypochlorite" title="Hypochlorite">hypochlorite</a> solutions, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferricyanide" title="Potassium ferricyanide">potassium ferricyanide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxides</a>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis_and_production">Synthesis and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Synthesis and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>
     </div> 
     <p><i>This section is about industrial synthesis. For synthesis in certain organisms, see section <a href="#Biosynthesis">¬ß‚ÄâBiosynthesis</a> below.</i></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Production_of_ammonia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Production_of_ammonia.svg/300px-Production_of_ammonia.svg.png" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Production_of_ammonia.svg/450px-Production_of_ammonia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Production_of_ammonia.svg/600px-Production_of_ammonia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="355"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Production_of_ammonia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Production trend of ammonia between 1947 and 2007
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Because of its many uses, ammonia is one of the most highly produced inorganic chemicals. Dozens of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_plant" title="Chemical plant">chemical plants</a> worldwide produce ammonia. Consuming more than 1% of all man-made power, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia production</a> is a significant component of the world energy budget.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-29">[29]</a></sup> The USGS reports global ammonia production in 2014 was 176 million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-10">[10]</a></sup> China accounted for 33% of that (increasingly from coal as part of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> synthesis), followed by Russia at 8.1%, India at 7.6%, and the United States at 6.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-10">[10]</a></sup> About 88% of the ammonia produced was used for fertilizing agricultural crops.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2016_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2016-10">[10]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Before the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, most ammonia was obtained by the <a href="/wiki/Dry_distillation" title="Dry distillation">dry distillation</a><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> of nitrogenous vegetable and animal waste products, including <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">dung</a>, where it was <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilled</a> by the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_acid" title="Nitrous acid">nitrous acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">nitrites</a> with hydrogen; in addition, it was produced by the distillation of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, and also by the decomposition of ammonium salts by <a href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline" class="mw-redirect">alkaline</a> hydroxides<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a>, the salt most generally used being the chloride (<a href="/wiki/Sal_ammoniac" title="Sal ammoniac">sal ammoniac</a>) thus:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       2 NH
       <sub>4</sub>Cl + 2 CaO ‚Üí CaCl
       <sub>2</sub> + Ca(OH)
       <sub>2</sub> + 2 NH
       <sub>3</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Hydrogen for ammonia synthesis could also be produced economically by using the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas" title="Water gas">water gas</a> reaction followed by the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift" title="Water gas shift" class="mw-redirect">water gas shift</a> reaction, produced by passing steam through red-hot <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>, to give a mixture of hydrogen and carbon dioxide gases, followed by removal of the carbon dioxide "washing" the gas mixture with water under pressure (25 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">standard atmospheres</a> (2,500&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>));<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> or by using other sources like coal or coke gasification.</p> 
     <p>Modern ammonia-producing plants depend on industrial <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">hydrogen production</a> to react with atmospheric nitrogen using a <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst" class="mw-redirect">catalyst</a> or over a promoted Fe catalyst under high pressure (100 standard atmospheres (10,000&nbsp;kPa)) and temperature (450&nbsp;¬∞C) to form anhydrous liquid ammonia. This step is known as the ammonia synthesis loop (also referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber‚ÄìBosch process</a>):<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       3 H
       <sub>2</sub> + N
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí 2 NH
       <sub>3</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Hydrogen required for ammonia synthesis could also be produced economically using other sources like coal or coke gasification or less economically from the electrolysis of water into oxygen + hydrogen and other alternatives that are presently impractical for large scale. At one time, most of Europe's ammonia was produced from the Hydro plant at <a href="/wiki/Vemork" title="Vemork">Vemork</a>, via the electrolysis route. Various renewable energy electricity sources are also potentially applicable.</p> 
     <p>As a sustainable alternative to the relatively inefficient <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, hydrogen can be generated from organic wastes (such as biomass or food-industry waste), using <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_reforming" title="Catalytic reforming">catalytic reforming</a>. This releases hydrogen from carbonaceous substances at only 10-20% of energy used by electrolysis and may lead to hydrogen being produced from municipal wastes at below zero cost (allowing for the tipping fees and efficient catalytic reforming, such as cold-plasma). Catalytic (thermal) reforming is possible in small, distributed (even mobile) plants, to take advantage of low-value, stranded biomass/biowaste or natural gas deposits. Conversion of such wastes into ammonia solves the problem of hydrogen storage, as hydrogen can be released economically from ammonia on-demand, without the need for high-pressure or cryogenic storage.</p> 
     <p>It is also easier to store ammonia on board vehicles than to store hydrogen, as ammonia is less flammable than petrol or LPG.</p> 
     <p>For small scale laboratory synthesis, one can heat urea and Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       (NH2)
       <sub>2</sub>CO + Ca(OH)
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí CaCO
       <sub>3</sub> + 2 NH
       <sub>3</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_ammonia_as_a_solvent">Liquid ammonia as a solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Liquid ammonia as a solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_nonaqueous_solvent" title="Inorganic nonaqueous solvent">Inorganic nonaqueous solvent</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Liquid ammonia is the best-known and most widely studied nonaqueous ionising solvent. Its most conspicuous property is its ability to dissolve alkali metals to form highly coloured, electrically conductive solutions containing <a href="/wiki/Solvated_electron" title="Solvated electron">solvated electrons</a>. Apart from these remarkable solutions, much of the chemistry in liquid ammonia can be classified by analogy with related reactions in aqueous solutions. Comparison of the physical properties of NH<sub>3</sub> with those of water shows NH<sub>3</sub> has the lower melting point, boiling point, density, <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant" class="mw-redirect">dielectric constant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity" class="mw-redirect">electrical conductivity</a>; this is due at least in part to the weaker hydrogen bonding in NH<sub>3</sub> and because such bonding cannot form cross-linked networks, since each NH<sub>3</sub> molecule has only one lone pair of electrons compared with two for each H<sub>2</sub>O molecule. The ionic self-<a href="/wiki/Dissociation_constant" title="Dissociation constant">dissociation constant</a> of liquid NH<sub>3</sub> at ‚àí50&nbsp;¬∞C is about 10<sup>‚àí33</sup> mol<sup>2</sup>¬∑l<sup>‚àí2</sup>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solubility_of_salts">Solubility of salts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Solubility of salts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <table> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <th></th> 
        <th>Solubility (g of salt per 100 g liquid NH<sub>3</sub>)</th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_acetate" title="Ammonium acetate">Ammonium acetate</a></td> 
        <td>253.2</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">Ammonium nitrate</a></td> 
        <td>389.6</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Lithium_nitrate" title="Lithium nitrate">Lithium nitrate</a></td> 
        <td>243.7</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">Sodium nitrate</a></td> 
        <td>97.6</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">Potassium nitrate</a></td> 
        <td>10.4</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">Sodium fluoride</a></td>

       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium chloride</a></td> 
        <td>157.0</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_bromide" title="Sodium bromide">Sodium bromide</a></td> 
        <td>138.0</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodide" title="Sodium iodide">Sodium iodide</a></td> 
        <td>161.9</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Sodium_thiocyanate" title="Sodium thiocyanate">Sodium thiocyanate</a></td> 
        <td>205.5</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>Liquid ammonia is an ionising solvent, although less so than water, and dissolves a range of ionic compounds, including many <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">nitrites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">thiocyanates</a>. Most ammonium salts are soluble and act as acids in liquid ammonia solutions. The solubility of <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halide</a> salts increases from <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a>. A saturated solution of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> contains 0.83&nbsp;mol solute per mole of ammonia and has a <a href="/wiki/Vapour_pressure" title="Vapour pressure" class="mw-redirect">vapour pressure</a> of less than 1&nbsp;bar even at 25&nbsp;¬∞C (77&nbsp;¬∞F).</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solutions_of_metals">Solutions of metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Solutions of metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Solvated_electron" title="Solvated electron">Solvated electron</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Liquid ammonia will dissolve the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electropositive</a> metals such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">ytterbium</a>. At low concentrations (&lt;0.06&nbsp;mol/l), deep blue solutions are formed: these contain metal cations and <a href="/wiki/Solvated_electron" title="Solvated electron">solvated electrons</a>, free electrons that are surrounded by a cage of ammonia molecules.</p> 
     <p>These solutions are very useful as strong reducing agents. At higher concentrations, the solutions are metallic in appearance and in electrical conductivity. At low temperatures, the two types of solution can coexist as <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/immiscible" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:immiscible">immiscible</a> phases.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redox_properties_of_liquid_ammonia">Redox properties of liquid ammonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Redox properties of liquid ammonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">Redox</a>
     </div> 
     <table cellpadding="5" style="text-align:center"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <th></th> 
        <th><a href="/wiki/Standard_electrode_potential" title="Standard electrode potential"><i>E</i>¬∞</a> (V, ammonia)</th> 
        <th><a href="/wiki/Standard_electrode_potential" title="Standard electrode potential"><i>E</i>¬∞</a> (V, water)</th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>Li<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå Li</td> 
        <td>‚àí2.24</td> 
        <td>‚àí3.04</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>K<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå K</td> 
        <td>‚àí1.98</td> 
        <td>‚àí2.93</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå Na</td> 
        <td>‚àí1.85</td> 
        <td>‚àí2.71</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>Zn<sup>2+</sup> + 2e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå Zn</td>

        <td>‚àí0.76</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> + e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå ¬Ω H<sub>2</sub> + NH<sub>3</sub></td> 
        <td>0.00</td> 
        <td>‚Äî</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 2e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå Cu</td> 
        <td>+0.43</td> 
        <td>+0.34</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td>Ag<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå Ag</td> 
        <td>+0.83</td> 
        <td>+0.80</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>The range of thermodynamic stability of liquid ammonia solutions is very narrow, as the potential for oxidation to dinitrogen, <a href="/wiki/Standard_electrode_potential" title="Standard electrode potential"><i>E</i>¬∞</a> (N<sub>2</sub> + 6NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> + 6e<sup>‚àí</sup> ‚áå 8NH<sub>3</sub>), is only +0.04&nbsp;V. In practice, both oxidation to dinitrogen and reduction to dihydrogen are slow. This is particularly true of reducing solutions: the solutions of the alkali metals mentioned above are stable for several days, slowly decomposing to the <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">metal amide</a> and dihydrogen. Most studies involving liquid ammonia solutions are done in reducing conditions; although oxidation of liquid ammonia is usually slow, there is still a risk of explosion, particularly if transition metal ions are present as possible catalysts.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ammonia.27s_role_in_biological_systems_and_human_disease">Ammonia's role in biological systems and human disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Ammonia's role in biological systems and human disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg/250px-Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg.png" width="250" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg/375px-Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg/500px-Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1151" data-file-height="1058"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_hyperammonemia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Main symptoms of hyperammonemia (ammonia reaching toxic concentrations).
        <sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia is both a <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">metabolic waste</a> and a metabolic input throughout the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. It is an important source of nitrogen for living systems. Although atmospheric nitrogen abounds (more than 75%), few living creatures are capable of using this atmospheric nitrogen in its diatomic form, N<sub>2</sub> gas. Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> is required for the synthesis of amino acids, which are the building blocks of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>. Some plants rely on ammonia and other nitrogenous wastes incorporated into the soil by decaying matter. Others, such as nitrogen-fixing <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>, benefit from <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> relationships with <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">rhizobia</a> that create ammonia from atmospheric nitrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis">Biosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Biosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In certain organisms, ammonia is produced from atmospheric nitrogen by <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenase" title="Nitrogenase">nitrogenases</a>. The overall process is called <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>. Although it is unlikely that biomimetic methods that are competitive with the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> will be developed,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> intense effort has been directed toward understanding the mechanism of biological nitrogen fixation. The scientific interest in this problem is motivated by the unusual structure of the active site of the enzyme, which consists of an Fe<sub>7</sub>MoS<sub>9</sub> ensemble.</p> 
     <p>Ammonia is also a metabolic product of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Deamination" title="Deamination">deamination</a> catalyzed by enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Glutamate_dehydrogenase_1#Function" title="Glutamate dehydrogenase 1">glutamate dehydrogenase 1</a>. Ammonia excretion is common in aquatic animals. In humans, it is quickly converted to <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, which is much less toxic, particularly less <a href="#Basicity">basic</a>. This urea is a major component of the dry weight of <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>. Most reptiles, birds, insects, and snails excrete <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> solely as nitrogenous waste.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_physiology">In physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: In physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Ammonia also plays a role in both normal and abnormal animal <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>. It is biosynthesised through normal amino acid metabolism and is toxic in high concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> converts ammonia to urea through a series of reactions known as the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>. Liver dysfunction, such as that seen in <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>, may lead to elevated amounts of ammonia in the blood (<a href="/wiki/Hyperammonemia" title="Hyperammonemia">hyperammonemia</a>). Likewise, defects in the enzymes responsible for the urea cycle, such as <a href="/wiki/Ornithine_transcarbamylase" title="Ornithine transcarbamylase">ornithine transcarbamylase</a>, lead to hyperammonemia. Hyperammonemia contributes to the confusion and <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_encephalopathy" title="Hepatic encephalopathy">hepatic encephalopathy</a>, as well as the neurologic disease common in people with urea cycle defects and <a href="/wiki/Organic_aciduria" title="Organic aciduria" class="mw-redirect">organic acidurias</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ammonia is important for normal animal acid/base balance. After formation of ammonium from <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/%CE%91-ketoglutarate" title="Œë-ketoglutarate" class="mw-redirect">Œ±-ketoglutarate</a> may be degraded to produce two molecules of <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>, which are then available as buffers for dietary acids. Ammonium is excreted in the urine, resulting in net acid loss. Ammonia may itself diffuse across the renal tubules, combine with a hydrogen ion, and thus allow for further acid excretion.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excretion">Excretion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Excretion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">Excretion</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonium ions are a <a href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic" class="mw-redirect">toxic</a> waste product of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>. In fish and aquatic invertebrates, it is excreted directly into the water. In mammals, sharks, and amphibians, it is converted in the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a> to <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, because it is less toxic and can be stored more efficiently. In birds, reptiles, and terrestrial snails, metabolic ammonium is converted into <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>, which is solid, and can therefore be excreted with minimal water loss.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:3508px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style=""> 
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png" class="image" title="Reference ranges for blood tests, comparing blood content of ammonia (shown in yellow near middle) with other constituents"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png/3500px-Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png" width="3500" height="198" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png 2x" data-file-width="4791" data-file-height="271"></a>
       </div> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests_-_by_molarity.png" title="File:Reference ranges for blood tests - by molarity.png"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests" title="Reference ranges for blood tests">Reference ranges for blood tests</a>, comparing blood content of ammonia (shown in yellow near middle) with other constituents
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_astronomy">In astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: In astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Jupiter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/220px-Jupiter.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/330px-Jupiter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/440px-Jupiter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="840"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Jupiter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Ammonia occurs in the 
        <a href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere" class="mw-redirect">atmospheres</a> of the outer gas planets such as 
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (0.026% ammonia) and 
        <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> (0.012% ammonia).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ammonia has been detected in the atmospheres of the <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> planets, including <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, along with other gases like methane, hydrogen, and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. The interior of Saturn may include frozen crystals of ammonia.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> It is naturally found on <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> ‚Äì the two <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">moons of Mars</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_space">Interstellar space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Interstellar space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Ammonia was first detected in interstellar space in 1968, based on <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> emissions from the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">galactic core</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> This was the first <a href="/wiki/Polyatomic" title="Polyatomic" class="mw-redirect">polyatomic</a> molecule to be so detected. The sensitivity of the molecule to a broad range of excitations and the ease with which it can be observed in a number of regions has made ammonia one of the most important molecules for studies of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ho_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ho-73">[73]</a></sup> The relative intensity of the ammonia lines can be used to measure the temperature of the emitting medium.</p> 
     <p>The following isotopic species of ammonia have been detected:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       NH
       <sub>3</sub>, 
       <sup>15</sup>NH
       <sub>3</sub>, NH
       <sub>2</sub>
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">D</a>, NHD
       <sub>2</sub>, and ND
       <sub>3</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The detection of triply <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterated</a> ammonia was considered a surprise as deuterium is relatively scarce. It is thought that the low-temperature conditions allow this molecule to survive and accumulate.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Since its interstellar discovery, NH<sub>3</sub> has proved to be an invaluable spectroscopic tool in the study of the interstellar medium. With a large number of transitions sensitive to a wide range of excitation conditions, NH<sub>3</sub> has been widely astronomically detected ‚Äì its detection has been reported in hundreds of journal articles. Listed below is a sample of journal articles that highlights the range of detectors that have been used to identify ammonia.</p> 
     <p>The study of interstellar ammonia has been important to a number of areas of research in the last few decades. Some of these are delineated below and primarily involve using ammonia as an interstellar thermometer.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_formation_mechanisms">Interstellar formation mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Interstellar formation mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Diamminesilver(I)-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/250px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png" width="250" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/375px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/500px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="602"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Diamminesilver(I)-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Ball-and-stick model of the diamminesilver(I) cation, [Ag(NH
        <sub>3</sub>)
        <sub>2</sub>]
        <sup>+</sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The interstellar abundance for ammonia has been measured for a variety of environments. The [NH<sub>3</sub>]/[H<sub>2</sub>] ratio has been estimated to range from 10<sup>‚àí7</sup> in small dark clouds<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> up to 10<sup>‚àí5</sup> in the dense core of the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Molecular_Cloud_Complex" title="Orion Molecular Cloud Complex">Orion Molecular Cloud Complex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Although a total of 18 total production routes have been proposed,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> the principal formation mechanism for interstellar NH<sub>3</sub> is the reaction:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       NH
       <sub>4</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> + e
       <sup>‚àí</sup> ‚Üí NH
       <sub>3</sub> + H¬∑
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The rate constant, <i>k</i>, of this reaction depends on the temperature of the environment, with a value of 5.2√ó10<sup>‚àí6</sup> at 10 K.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The rate constant was calculated from the formula <i>k = a(T/300)<sup>B</sup></i>. For the primary formation reaction, <i>a</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.05√ó10<sup>‚àí6</sup> and <i>B</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;‚àí0.47. Assuming an NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> abundance of 3√ó10<sup>‚àí7</sup> and an electron abundance of 10<sup>‚àí7</sup> typical of molecular clouds, the formation will proceed at a rate of 1.6√ó10<sup>‚àí9</sup> cm<sup>‚àí3</sup>s<sup>‚àí1</sup> in a molecular cloud of total density 10<sup>5</sup> cm<sup>‚àí3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>All other proposed formation reactions have rate constants of between 2 and 13 orders of magnitude smaller, making their contribution to the abundance of ammonia relatively insignificant.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> As an example of the minor contribution other formation reactions play, the reaction:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       H
       <sub>2</sub> + NH
       <sub>2</sub> ‚Üí NH
       <sub>3</sub> + H
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>has a rate constant of 2.2√ó10<sup>‚àí15</sup>. Assuming H<sub>2</sub> densities of 10<sup>5</sup> and NH<sub>2</sub>/H<sub>2</sub> ratio of 10<sup>‚àí7</sup>, this reaction proceeds at a rate of 2.2√ó10<sup>‚àí12</sup>, more than 3 orders of magnitude slower than the primary reaction above.</p> 
     <p>Some of the other possible formation reactions are:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       H
       <sup>‚àí</sup> + NH
       <sub>4</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> ‚Üí NH
       <sub>3</sub> + H
       <sub>2</sub>
      </dd> 
      <dd>
       PNH
       <sub>3</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> + e
       <sup>‚àí</sup> ‚Üí P + NH
       <sub>3</sub>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_destruction_mechanisms">Interstellar destruction mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Interstellar destruction mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>There are 113 total proposed reactions leading to the destruction of NH<sub>3</sub>. Of these, 39 were tabulated in extensive tables of the chemistry among C, N, and O compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> A review of interstellar ammonia cites the following reactions as the principal dissociation mechanisms:<sup id="cite_ref-ho_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ho-73">[73]</a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       (1) NH
       <sub>3</sub> + H
       <sub>3</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> ‚Üí NH
       <sub>4</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> + H
       <sub>2</sub>
      </dd> 
      <dd>
       (2) NH
       <sub>3</sub> + HCO
       <sup>+</sup> ‚Üí NH
       <sub>4</sub>
       <sup>+</sup> + CO
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>with rate constants of 4.39√ó10<sup>‚àí9</sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and 2.2√ó10<sup>‚àí9</sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> respectively. The above equations (1,2) run at a rate of 8.8√ó10<sup>‚àí9</sup> and 4.4√ó10<sup>‚àí13</sup>, respectively. These calculations assumed the given rate constants and abundances of [NH<sub>3</sub>]/[H<sub>2</sub>] = 10<sup>‚àí5</sup>, [H<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup>]/[H<sub>2</sub>] = 2√ó10<sup>‚àí5</sup>, [HCO<sup>+</sup>]/[H<sub>2</sub>] = 2√ó10<sup>‚àí9</sup>, and total densities of n = 10<sup>5</sup>, typical of cold, dense, molecular clouds.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Clearly, between these two primary reactions, equation (1) is the dominant destruction reaction, with a rate ~10,000 times faster than equation (2). This is due to the relatively high abundance of H<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_antenna_detections">Single antenna detections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Single antenna detections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Radio observations of NH<sub>3</sub> from the <a href="/wiki/Effelsberg_100-m_Radio_Telescope" title="Effelsberg 100-m Radio Telescope">Effelsberg 100-m Radio Telescope</a> reveal that the ammonia line is separated into two components ‚Äì a background ridge and an unresolved core. The background corresponds well with the locations previously detected CO.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The 25 m Chilbolton telescope in England detected radio signatures of ammonia in <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a>, HNH<sub>2</sub>O <a href="/wiki/Maser" title="Maser">masers</a>, H-H objects, and other objects associated with star formation. A comparison of emission line widths indicates that turbulent or systematic velocities do not increase in the central cores of molecular clouds.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Microwave radiation from ammonia was observed in several galactic objects including W3(OH), <a href="/wiki/Orion_(constellation)" title="Orion (constellation)">Orion A</a>, W43, W51, and five sources in the galactic centre. The high detection rate indicates that this is a common molecule in the interstellar medium and that high-density regions are common in the galaxy.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interferometric_studies">Interferometric studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Interferometric studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Array" title="Very Large Array" class="mw-redirect">VLA</a> observations of NH<sub>3</sub> in seven regions with high-velocity gaseous outflows revealed condensations of less than 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> in L1551, S140, and <a href="/wiki/Cepheus_(constellation)" title="Cepheus (constellation)">Cepheus A</a>. Three individual condensations were detected in Cepheus A, one of them with a highly elongated shape. They may play an important role in creating the bipolar outflow in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Extragalactic ammonia was imaged using the VLA in <a href="/wiki/IC_342" title="IC 342">IC 342</a>. The hot gas has temperatures above 70 K, which was inferred from ammonia line ratios and appears to be closely associated with the innermost portions of the nuclear bar seen in CO.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> NH<sub>3</sub> was also monitored by VLA toward a sample of four galactic ultracompact HII regions: G9.62+0.19, G10.47+0.03, G29.96‚àí0.02, and G31.41+0.31. Based upon temperature and density diagnostics, it is concluded that in general such clumps are likely to be the sites of massive star formation in an early evolutionary phase prior to the development of an ultracompact HII region.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared_detections">Infrared detections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Infrared detections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Absorption at 2.97 micrometres due to solid ammonia was recorded from interstellar grains in the <a href="/wiki/Becklin-Neugebauer_Object" title="Becklin-Neugebauer Object" class="mw-redirect">Becklin-Neugebauer Object</a> and probably in NGC 2264-IR as well. This detection helped explain the physical shape of previously poorly understood and related ice absorption lines.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A spectrum of the disk of Jupiter was obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_Airborne_Observatory" title="Kuiper Airborne Observatory">Kuiper Airborne Observatory</a>, covering the 100 to 300&nbsp;cm<sup>‚àí1</sup> spectral range. Analysis of the spectrum provides information on global mean properties of ammonia gas and an ammonia ice haze.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A total of 149 dark cloud positions were surveyed for evidence of 'dense cores' by using the (J,K) = (1,1) rotating inversion line of NH<sub>3</sub>. In general, the cores are not spherically shaped, with aspect ratios ranging from 1.1 to 4.4. It is also found that cores with stars have broader lines than cores without stars.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ammonia has been detected in the <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">Draco Nebula</a> and in one or possibly two molecular clouds, which are associated with the high-latitude galactic <a href="/wiki/Infrared_cirrus" title="Infrared cirrus">infrared cirrus</a>. The finding is significant because they may represent the birthplaces for the Population I metallicity B-type stars in the galactic halo that could have been borne in the galactic disk.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_of_nearby_dark_clouds">Observations of nearby dark clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Observations of nearby dark clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>By balancing and stimulated emission with spontaneous emission, it is possible to construct a relation between <a href="/wiki/Excitation_temperature" title="Excitation temperature">excitation temperature</a> and density. Moreover, since the transitional levels of ammonia can be approximated by a 2-level system at low temperatures, this calculation is fairly simple. This premise can be applied to dark clouds, regions suspected of having extremely low temperatures and possible sites for future star formation. Detections of ammonia in dark clouds show very narrow lines‚Äîindicative not only of low temperatures, but also of a low level of inner-cloud turbulence. Line ratio calculations provide a measurement of cloud temperature that is independent of previous CO observations. The ammonia observations were consistent with CO measurements of rotation temperatures of ~10 K. With this, densities can be determined, and have been calculated to range between 10<sup>4</sup> and 10<sup>5</sup> cm<sup>‚àí3</sup> in dark clouds. Mapping of NH<sub>3</sub> gives typical clouds sizes of 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> and masses near 1 solar mass. These cold, dense cores are the sites of future star formation.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UC_HII_regions">UC HII regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: UC HII regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Ultra-compact HII regions are among the best tracers of high-mass star formation. The dense material surrounding UCHII regions is likely primarily molecular. Since a complete study of massive star formation necessarily involves the cloud from which the star formed, ammonia is an invaluable tool in understanding this surrounding molecular material. Since this molecular material can be spatially resolved, it is possible to constrain the heating/ionising sources, temperatures, masses, and sizes of the regions. Doppler-shifted velocity components allow for the separation of distinct regions of molecular gas that can trace outflows and hot cores originating from forming stars.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Extragalactic_detection">Extragalactic detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Extragalactic detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Ammonia has been detected in external galaxies, and by simultaneously measuring several lines, it is possible to directly measure the gas temperature in these galaxies. Line ratios imply that gas temperatures are warm (~50 K), originating from dense clouds with sizes of tens of pc. This picture is consistent with the picture within our <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy‚Äîhot dense molecular cores form around newly forming stars embedded in larger clouds of molecular material on the scale of several hundred pc (giant molecular clouds; GMCs).</p>
